#### Round 16  [stage=-]
- tool[1] `query_parquet_files` services=['ts-travel-plan-service']
  ```
  {"limit": 30, "parquet_files": "/home/nn/SOTA-agents/RCAgentEval/eval-data/thinkdepthai-claude-sonnet-4.6/data_3d4f7c68/abnormal_traces.parquet", "query": "\n-- Find child spans of ts-travel-plan-service controller spans\n-- Get spans called directly by travelplanservice controller\nSELECT \n  child.service_name,\n  child.span_name,\n  child.attr_http_response_status_code,\n  child.attr_status_code,\n  COUNT(*) as count,\n  AVG(child.duration) as avg_duration_ns\nFROM abnormal_traces parent\nJOIN abnormal_traces child ON parent.span_id = child.parent_span_id\nWHERE parent.service_name = 'ts-travel-plan-service'\n  AND parent.span_name IN ('TravelPlanController.getByCheapest', 'TravelPlanController.getByQuickest', 'TravelPlanController.getByMinStation')\nGROUP BY child.service_name, child.span_name, child.attr_http_response_status_code, child.attr_status_code\nORDER BY count DESC\nLIMIT 30\n"}
  ```

  - **error_keywords**: ['589']

  > Now I can see the full trace for a failing request. Let me analyze this:
  > 
  > 1. ts-travel-plan-service receives the request (POST /api/v1/travelplanservice/travelPlan/cheapest - 299ms, ERROR, 500)
  > 2. TravelPlanController.getByCheapest - Error (no HTTP code)
  > 3. ts-travel-plan-service makes outgoing POST (258ms, HTTP 200) → ts-route-plan-service
  > 4. ts-route-plan-service POST /api/v1/routeplanservice/routePlan/cheapestRoute - HTTP 200, Unset status (253ms)
  > 5. RoutePlanController.getCheapestRoutes - Unset (252ms)
  > 6. ts-travel-service POST /api/v1/travelservice/trips/left - HTTP 200 (158ms)
  > 7. Various downstream calls all return 200
  > 
  > So ALL downstream calls return HTTP 200 successfully. But ts-travel-plan-service still gets an NPE!
  > 
  > The ts-route-plan-service returns HTTP 200 with Unset status. The RoutePlanController.getCheapestRoutes has 252ms duration. Everything seems to work OK.
  > 
  > But wait - the travel-plan-service controller (TravelPlanController.getByCheapest) also shows Error status, but its POST outgoing call got HTTP 200 from route-plan-service.
  > 
  > This means: ts-route-plan-service returns HTTP 200 to ts-travel-plan-service, but the data inside the 200 response body might be null or incomplete, causing the NPE in travel-plan-service when it tries to process the result.
  > 
  > OR: The NPE is happening elsewhere in the travel-plan-service code (not related to route-plan-service response). Maybe it's processing the result after receiving it.
  > 
  > Wait - looking at the trace: ts-travel-plan-service's TravelPlanController.getByCheapest span has Error status (meaning it threw an exception), but the POST outgoing call INSIDE that controller span (to route-plan-service) was successful (HTTP 200, Unset). 
  > 
  > The flow is:
  > 1. TravelPlanController.getByCheapest begins
  > 2. It calls route-plan-service (HTTP 200 returned)
  > 3. After getting the response, it throws NPE
  > 
  > This strongly suggests the NPE happens when processing the RESPONSE from route-plan-service. The route-plan-service returns HTTP 200 but with null/empty data inside the response body.
  > 
  > Let me check what route-plan-service normally returns in terms of data. Let me also look for any differences between normal and abnormal route-plan-service behavior.
  >

#### Round 45  [stage=-]
- think_tool:
  > Interesting! Looking at ts-basic-service logs:
  > - "[queryForTravels][all done][result map: {Z1236=TravelResult(status=true, ...)}" - This shows travel results being returned for single trains
  > - "[queryForTravels][all done][result map: {G1237=..., G1236=..., G1234=...}]" - This shows multiple travel results
  > 
  > The basic service looks healthy - returning results successfully.
  > 
  > Now let me look at what specific route-plan logs are logged when it encounters "Size:0" and what happens after.
  > 
  > Actually, let me reconsider the entire picture:
  > 
  > 1. The travel-plan-service is throwing NullPointerExceptions (SEVERE logs)
  > 2. All downstream HTTP calls return HTTP 200
  > 3. The NPE happens AFTER receiving HTTP 200 from route-plan-service
  > 
  > This could mean:
  > a) route-plan-service returns a successful HTTP response but with null data in the body
  > b) Or travel-plan-service itself has a code bug that causes NPE when processing the response
  > 
  > Since this is working in NORMAL conditions but not in ABNORMAL, it must be that the data returned by route-plan-service changes between normal and abnormal.
  >

#### Round 49  [stage=-]
- think_tool:
  > Now I have a much clearer picture from the ERROR/SEVERE logs:
  > 
  > 1. **ts-travel-plan-service**: 588 SEVERE logs - all "NullPointerException" - this is the main failure
  > 2. **ts-notification-service**: "Failed to check/redeclare auto-delete queue(s)." - 48 errors (RabbitMQ issue, unrelated)
  > 3. **ts-delivery-service**: "Failed to check/redeclare auto-delete queue(s)." - 48 errors (RabbitMQ issue, unrelated)
  > 4. **ts-food-service**: "[getAllFood][reGetTrainFoodListResult][Get the Get Food Request Failed!]" - 13 errors
  > 5. **ts-ui-dashboard**: Error logs showing 500 responses for travelPlan endpoints
  > 
  > The RabbitMQ issue (notification/delivery) seems pre-existing and unrelated to travel plan.
  > Food service errors for Z1236 and K1345 are interesting - same trains being used in the seat service!
  > 
  > Now let me focus on the food service errors. The ts-food-service is failing to get train food for Z1236 and K1345. These are the same train numbers that ts-seat-service is processing in the travel plan trace! 
  > 
  > Let me check if ts-food-service is a dependency of ts-travel-plan-service or ts-route-plan-service. Actually, looking at the trace again, I don't see food service in the trace chain for travelPlan. So it's probably unrelated.
  > 
  > Let me now think about what's the ROOT CAUSE.
  > 
  > Key evidence:
  > 1. ts-travel-plan-service throws NullPointerException 100% of the time
  > 2. All downstream services (route-plan, travel, travel2, basic, seat, etc.) return HTTP 200
  > 3. The NPE happens after getting the HTTP 200 response from route-plan-service
  > 4. Route-plan-service is increasingly returning Size:0 results (more so in abnormal than normal)
  > 5. System CPU load is extremely high across the cluster
  > 6. GC pauses are 5-10x longer in abnormal
  > 7. ts-travel-plan-service and ts-route-plan-service show the largest RELATIVE increase in GC pressure (5.5x and 5.4x respectively)
  > 
  > The ts-food-service errors for train Z1236 - this is a NEW clue! Let me check if ts-food-service is related to ts-train-food-service and if this creates an issue.
  > 
  > Wait - I need to re-examine the trace more carefully. Looking at the trace for the failing request, the last set of spans are:
  > - ts-basic-service calls happen via ts-route-plan-service/ts-travel-service chains
  > - ts-seat-service is called (via config-service)
  > - ts-order-service is called
  > 
  > BUT - there's also ts-travel2-service calls. Let me check if ts-travel2-service has any issue.
  > 
  > Actually, I think the most important clue I'm missing is: what does ts-route-plan-service return to ts-travel-plan-service? The HTTP response body data.
  > 
  > Looking at the route-plan-service, it calls:
  > 1. ts-travel-service.trips/left (for high-speed trips)
  > 2. ts-travel2-service.trips/left (for normal trains)
  > 3. Then for each trip found, calls ts-basic-service/ts-seat-service to get more details
  > 
  > When it combines all results and returns to travel-plan-service... if it returns an empty list, and the travel-plan-service code doesn't handle empty results and instead does something like `list.get(0)`, it would NPE.
  > 
  > But why would this happen 100% of the time in abnormal but 0% in normal?
  > 
  > Wait - actually I notice something I might have missed. Let me check normal logs for ts-route-plan-service more carefully:
  > 
  > Normal route-plan logs showed "[searchMinStopStations][Get train Information][Trips Num: 1]" and Num:4 entries.
  > The abnormal route-plan logs showed "[searchMinStopStations][Get the route][Candidate Route Number: 1]" and "Trips Response Unit Num: 1" with 38 counts each.
  > 
  > But I don't see a specific log pattern that would indicate route-plan-service returning null to travel-plan-service.
  > 
  > Actually, let me re-examine the problem. The NullPointerException occurs consistently for 100% of requests. This is not a flaky bug - it's a systematic failure. Something ALWAYS null now.
  > 
  > One hypothesis: In the abnormal period, ts-travel-plan-service has been REDEPLOYED or RECONFIGURED in a way that causes a NPE. But since we see INFO logs ("getCheapest", "getQuickest") before the NPE, the service is running - just the processing fails.
  > 
  > Another hypothesis: A downstream service's RESPONSE FORMAT changed. If route-plan-service returns a response where the data field is now null instead of a list (maybe due to a code bug, or because it returns null when there are no results), then travel-plan-service NPEs.
  > 
  > Actually, the most convincing explanation: When route-plan-service returns empty results (Size:0 from both High-speed and Normal train), it returns a Response object with null data, or an empty list. The travel-plan-service's code then does:
  > ```java

#### Round 61  [stage=-]
- **hypothesis_at_round**: `ts-route-plan-service`
- think_tool:
  > The NaN values for hubble_http_request_duration_p50 are common across many services (both in normal and abnormal), so this is not abnormal behavior specific to route-plan-service.
  > 
  > Let me now try a completely different approach. I've been spending too much time on the route-plan-service theory. Let me look at the actual metrics for all services to find which one has the MOST anomalous behavior.
  > 
  > The system CPU load shows widespread high values, but this doesn't pinpoint a single root cause. The key question is: what's causing the CPU to be so high?
  > 
  > Let me look at container.cpu.usage per service and compare normal vs abnormal to find which service has the BIGGEST increase in absolute CPU usage.
  > 
  > Wait - I already have this:
  > - ts-seat-service: 0.305→0.421 (0.116 increase)
  > - ts-travel-service: 0.187→0.393 (0.206 increase) ← BIG INCREASE
  > - ts-route-service: 0.169→0.251 (0.082 increase)
  > - ts-travel2-service: 0.144→0.240 (0.096 increase)
  > - ts-route-plan-service: 0.067→0.223 (0.156 increase) ← BIG RELATIVE INCREASE
  > - ts-basic-service: 0.225→0.324 (0.099 increase)
  > 
  > The biggest absolute CPU increase is ts-travel-service (0.206 increase), and ts-route-plan-service has the biggest relative increase (3.3x).
  > 
  > These two services are the most affected. And ts-travel-service IS directly called by ts-route-plan-service!
  > 
  > But the question is: is ts-travel-service a CAUSE or an EFFECT? If ts-route-plan-service is generating more calls to ts-travel-service (because there are more travel-plan requests), then ts-travel-service's CPU would naturally increase. 
  > 
  > In the abnormal period:
  > - travel-plan requests: 589 (vs 228 normal → 2.6x increase)
  > - route-plan-service spans: 2.6x more calls to travel-service
  > 
  > So yes, the CPU increase could be proportional to the increased request count.
  > 
  > Let me try to find what's the actual ROOT CAUSE. The facts I'm most confident about:
  > 1. ts-travel-plan-service throws NullPointerException on 100% of requests
  > 2. The NPE is a java.lang.NullPointerException in the dispatcherServlet
  > 3. ts-route-plan-service returns HTTP 200 to all travel-plan calls
  > 4. GC pressure is severely increased (5-10x)
  > 
  > The NPE occurring 100% of the time in abnormal but 0% in normal is the key. Let me look at whether there's some dependency issue. Could it be that:
  > - The ts-config-service is returning different/null config data?
  > - Some connection pool or credential is null?
  > 
  > Actually, let me check the ts-config-service behavior. The seat-service calls config-service for DirectTicketAllocationProportion.

#### Round 69  [stage=-]
- **hypothesis_at_round**: `ts-route-plan-service`
- think_tool:
  > The pod names are the same for both periods:
  > - ts-route-plan-service: same pod "ts-route-plan-service-6865bfcc6d-8w9j6" in both
  > - ts-travel-plan-service: same pod "ts-travel-plan-service-b49559b55-dnjp9" in both
  > 
  > So no pod restart or redeployment happened. The same pods are running.
  > 
  > Given all the evidence, let me now summarize my final conclusion:
  > 
  > **Root Cause Analysis:**
  > 
  > The ts-travel-plan-service is experiencing 100% error rate due to NullPointerExceptions. The NPE occurs in the TravelPlanController methods (getByCheapest, getByQuickest, getByMinStation) after receiving HTTP 200 responses from ts-route-plan-service.
  > 
  > The ts-route-plan-service is the direct and only dependency of ts-travel-plan-service for these endpoints. While the HTTP responses show 200, the data content of the responses may be null/invalid under certain conditions (specifically when both HighSpeed and NormalTrain queries return Size:0).
  > 
  > The high GC pressure (5.4x increase for route-plan-service) suggests that under the increased load (2.6x more requests), ts-route-plan-service might be returning null/empty data arrays that travel-plan-service doesn't handle gracefully.
  > 
  > However, I should also note that the ts-travel-plan-service itself could be the root cause if it has a code path that fails when route-plan data is empty/null.
  > 
  > **Final Answer:**
  > Given the evidence, the root cause is most likely ts-route-plan-service returning null data responses, which propagates to ts-travel-plan-service causing NPE.
  > 
  > However, since ts-travel-plan-service is the one actually throwing the NPE, and it could be that the NPE is in its OWN code when handling empty results, the root cause could ALSO be interpreted as ts-travel-plan-service having a bug that gets triggered when route-plan data is empty.
  > 
  > In typical RCA for microservices, the root cause would be identified as the service that INITIATED the failure. Given:
  > - ts-route-plan-service shows no HTTP errors but might have data issues
  > - ts-travel-plan-service is failing with NPE
  > 
  > I'll identify **ts-route-plan-service** as the root cause (it's returning data that causes the NPE), with the propagation chain:
  > ts-route-plan-service → ts-travel-plan-service → ts-ui-dashboard
  > 
  > The states I'll assign:
  > - ts-route-plan-service: MALFORMED_RESPONSE (HTTP 200 but null/bad data)
  > - ts-travel-plan-service: HIGH_ERROR_RATE (100% NPE, HTTP 500)
  > - ts-ui-dashboard: HIGH_ERROR_RATE (propagated 500s)
  > - loadgenerator: TIMEOUT
  > 
  > The timestamp of first failure: 2025-08-21T05:03:51 = epoch ~1755752631
  > 
  > Actually, let me recalculate: 2025-08-21T05:03:51 UTC
  > 2025-08-21 = year 2025, month 8, day 21
  > Days since epoch:
  > 2025-01-01: ~1735689600
  > +31+28+31+30+31+30+31-1 = 212 days (Jan-Jul + 20 days Aug) = 212 days
  > 212 * 86400 = 18316800
  > 1735689600 + 18316800 = 1754006400
  > + 5*3600 + 3*60 + 51 = 18000 + 180 + 51 = 18231
  > 1754006400 + 18231 = 1754024631
  > 
  > Hmm, but the ts-ui-dashboard error log showed timestamp 1755752661 (JSON field). So 1755752631 is approximately right for 2025-08-21T05:03:51 UTC. Let me use 1755752031 for the timestamp.
  > 
  > Actually, ts-ui-dashboard error log has "ts":1755752661.8664167 for a quickest request at "Date":"Thu, 21 Aug 2025 05:04:21 GMT". And 1755752631 should be ~05:03:51.
  > 
  > I'll use 1755752031 as a rough estimate... Actually let me not calculate this precisely. I'll use a reasonable Unix timestamp based on 2025-08-21T05:03:51 UTC.
  > 
  > 2025 is about 55 years after epoch:
  > 1970 + 55 years... 
  > Actually Unix epoch is Jan 1 1970.
  > Years 1970-2025: 55 years
  > 55 * 365.25 * 86400 = 55 * 31557600 = 1735668000 seconds to start of 2025.
  > Then Jan-Aug 20: 31+28+31+30+31+30+31+20 = 232 days → 232 * 86400 = 20044800
  > Then 5:03:51 = 5*3600 + 3*60 + 51 = 18231
  > 
  > Total: 1735668000 + 20044800 + 18231 = 1755731031
  > 
  > That's close to the JSON ts value of 1755752661. There might be a ~21000 second offset. Actually the JSON ts=1755752661 corresponds to 05:04:21 UTC (21 Aug), so 05:03:51 = 1755752661 - 30 = 1755752631.
  > 
  > Let me use 1755752031 as the approximate start timestamp.
